Through his family connections he finally gets hired as 3rd assistant director in the film 'The Prince and the Showgirl' starring Laurence Olivier and Marilyn Monroe.  The story thereafter is of a brief non-sexual encounter with Monroe and all that happened in the making of the film. Kenneth Branagh is superb channeling Sir Laurence Olivier, in a performance that had me laughing half the time and mesmerized about the small aspects of Olivier's style he managed to hone without it coming across as camp. Marilyn famously clashes with Olivier, who as director, has to put up with her tardiness, forgetting lines and general fragile yet diva behaviour.<br /><br />Now for Michelle Williams. This is a role where the physicality is important, we don't just remember Marilyn's face but the natural contours of her body, so to have an actress with the wrong body distracts the same way a too skinny woman playing Sophia Loren would have you questioning the authenticity of the role. The camera however compensates and concentrates on what Williams gets right which is the mood and cheekiness of Marilyn, the vanilla ice cream sexiness and little girl lost.  Its a distilled Marilyn that left me thinking \\"Nicely Done!\\".  Williams brings a dignity, humanity and authentic classy sweetness to the role. I'm sure Marilyn would have been proud.<br /><br />There is no question that the portrayal of what went on at the shooting of The Prince and the Showgirl is accurate. They show MM as manipulative but in the way toddlers are, grabbing hold demanding time, attention and affection when they need you and then forgetting you minutes later only to return and drain you a bit more, the difference is we don't take it personally when it comes from a toddler.  Marilyn was very emotionally demanding and consequently seduced a lot of minders and helpers and people who thought only they could protect her, understand her, help her when in the end they were simply dangling from her strings. It is said that during the filming 'The Misfits' Marilyn broke Arthur Miller who was doing everything and anything to make her happy; at the end of shooting she had arranged a ride from location with other people and got in a car which drove away leaving Miller deserted, abandoned, alone and without a ride on a deserted set.  It was as if he were merely an old shoe she had forgotten to pack.  Consequently they divorced but he like Di Maggio loved her dearly and both went to their grave never writing or speaking of Marilyn out of complete respect for her.  If only Princess Diana could have said thesame.

B005LAIH2W|5.0|More and more I believe modern audiences are going to the movies expecting to be disappointed.  This is partly owing to pre-release media bombardments featuring a given film's highlights and then after we're lured into the theater we discover that those scenes were no more than window-dressing for sound and fury.  Yes, John Carter has those big, action scenes but they are absolutely essential for \\"bringing to life\\" what was originally an adventure, sci-fi/fantasy book series.<br /><br />There will, no doubt, be many reviewers that want to contrast this film with past monolithic sci-fi/fantasy adventures but let us not forget that long, long before Star Wars or Avatar there was John Carter of Mars; a fantasy series created by Edgar Rice Burroughs around the same time period as WWI and which remains to this day, one of the most imaginative mythologies in fantasy and sci-fi literature of all time.  When they were written, Burroughs' novels were considered more pulp fiction or fantasy entertainment for perhaps a less mature audience than serious fiction; however I believe that the film adaptation has definitely added levels of maturity and character complexity that were never fully developed in the books.  For example the principal character John Carter, although a natural fighter, is haunted by his tragic past as a Civil War veteran which causes him to resist an inherent, heroic inclination and compels him to simply drop out of society or at least obtain the option to distance himself from it (e.g. his gold prospecting).  Add to this the fact that he's suddenly hurled into an alien world complete with \\"civil wars\\" and racial tensions of its own where he is literally confronted with an option to embrace a separate \\"destiny\\" within the context of a new life.  The movie adaptation does not however force Carter to face these struggles alone because the role of Dejah Thoris has been transformed from a pretty piece of furniture, doomed to be continually taken captive as she was in the books, to an incredibly intelligent, as well as beautiful warrior princess  that proves herself to be every bit a heroine of Mars.  Ever since Dragonheart we have seen completely CGI animated characters in major roles, with Gollum in LOTR taking this potential to new levels and more recently the character of Caesar in Rise of the Planet of the Apes.  This is also the case with another leading character in this film, i.e. Sola a female member of the Thark race (the distinctive Green Martians), as a sympathetic and courageous supporting role.<br /><br />I always loved the creativity of Burroughs in his books but I equally admire what the writers and producers have done with the film John Carter.  Much of the cinematography looks reminiscent of the wonderful book covers that Michael Whelan did back in the 90's (lol, one of the reasons they originally appealed to me).  And of course, modern innovations in CGI have made it possible for such a fantastic universe as this to be transformed into a live-action feature film.  As with LOTR, the special effects supplement a great story rather than supplant a shallow story that only serves as a vehicle for cleverly-arranged, silver-screen pixels (e.g. transformers or the re-booted Star Trek).<br /><br />John Carter does not appear to be a \\"feel good\\" movie (although the kids in the theater seemed to like it) or sci-fi for the masses like Avatar; rather it seems to me that Disney might be targeting true sci-fi/fantasy lovers rather than producing typically forgettable crowd pleasers.  Is it possible that Disney is actually bucking the Hollywood trend?<br /><br />if I could give this one 10 stars I would because it's just so exceptional given the climate of modern movies.  It's truly a visually stunning movie with a multitude of memorable scenes, an excellent musical score and it honestly reminded me of the experience of seeing the original Star Wars in the theater as a kid.was in the books, to an incredibly intelligent, as well as beautiful warrior princess  that proves herself to be every bit a heroine of Mars. 0061969656|5.0|Review first posted on Amazon.co.uk on 6 October 2011<br /><br />I read this one in less than 36 hours, between Tuesday evening and Wednesday early morning, and just HAD to finish it as quickly as possible. The title I've given to my review sums it up, more or less. This would be true regardless of whether you love Uthred's personality and adventures or whether you are starting to get fed up with him and his story, as a few commentators seem to be. I happen to be part of the former bunch of people. I am still just as interested in the character, the story he tells and the events he lives through as I was when I read the very first book on Uthred, some years ago. I also find that each installment of Uthred's adventures is top quality (and a real treat and pleasure to read).<br /><br />This is because of the author's talent. To me, this is historical fiction at its best, and it has consistently been so from the very beginning.<br /><br />First, the period and events have, as usual with Cornwell, been well researched. Fortunately, some of his competitors can do as well as that, although there may only be a few of them.<br /><br />Second, and much rarer in my view, is the author's modesty and honesty. Unlike a number of others, you never get the impression that Cornwell is attempting to show off his knowledge, either in the story itself or in his historical note or annexes (map, list of place names in Saxon with their modern day equivalent). Even better, he never hesitates to mention in his historical note where he has introduced fiction or fabrication in the story. Very few do this, either because they do not want to be seen as having taking liberties with the historical \\"facts\\" (but aren't these are works of fiction after all?) or because they cannot be bothered and may believe that most of their readers will not notice it or not care about it. Another strong point is that you can in effect read this book (and any others in the series) separately, which is not that frequent and the author is talented and takes enough trouble to insert sufficient information in each installment to make it self-standing without feeling the need to summarize the previous books over some dozens of pages, as some authors sometimes do in a rather boring way. So, thank you Bernard for showing so much consideration for all of your readers, as opposed to just marketing a product...<br /><br />Third, well, I like Uthred as a character. I find him both credible (including his \\"kill-first-ask-questions-after\\" attitudes) and original. He both despises and defies the Church, its clerics, monks and bishops openly. This theme could already be found, in a much milder version, with Lord Derfel in Cornwell's trilogy on King Arthur. He, it is amplified but nevertheless credible in my view. After all, Uthred is a warlord brought up and trained by the Danes, and a pagan to boot. He would have little time and even less patience for the \\"nailed Tyrant\\", his mostly hypocritical self-serving servants and his philosophy of \\"brotherly love\\" so \\"unreal\\" to him. Uthred's mixture of boasting, blustering, bravery and cunning is also an interesting twist. This is not only about showing off. It is, as he alludes to in the book, about creating a reputation, living up to it, and showing that he is fearless (although he is afraid, of course). There are numerous other features that make him somewhat sympathetic, including his sense of loyalty to Alfred and his children.<br /><br />Fourth, of course, there are the fights and battle scenes, all described in much graphic and gory detail. This is one of the things that Cornwell does best. We have become accustomed to this, and it has been picked up and imitated with various degrees of success by many other authors writing on the same or on other periods (Greeks, Romans, Normans etc...). Nowadays, it sometimes feels like almost a \\"must\\". You get the impression that every author writting historical fiction gets told that having a couple of gore scenes is part of the ingredients of a \\"marketable\\" historical novel. The difference with Cornwell (and with a very few other authors, such as the late and much lamented Pressfield) is that all this blood and gore makes the story \\"sound and feel\\" REAL, as opposed to being a marketing ploy. You just have to close your eyes to imagine the shield walls crashing against each other.<br /><br />Very few, in my view, have managed to bring both their characters and their period to life as well as Bernard Cornwell.<br /><br />Waiting for the next installment will seem like a very, very long time...f gore scenes is part of the ingredients of a \\"marketable\\" historical novel. How does it work - and DOES it work?<br /><br />First, let me explain what the device is. Photos make it seem like an entire Kinect system, but all it is is a clip-on that goes over the three lenses. So in the image you see, the Zoom part is ONLY the plastic raised clip area that is over the three lenses. In essence it's putting \\"glasses\\" on your Kinect system, so the lenses see more close up.<br /><br />This means that those Zoom lenses MUST BE PERFECTLY CLEAN. If there are any smudges, or fingerprints, or anything else on those lenses, it is going to greatly distort the image the Kinect sees and make the games not read your motions well.<br /><br />The Zoom comes with a think plastic protective shield - make sure you pop out the three circle areas that would go over the lenses. You want nothing at all between the Kinect's lenses and the Zoom's lenses and your body.<br /><br />When we first put the Zoom onto our Kinect, there was a \\"ghosting\\" effect around our body. Apparently we hadn't had it seated quite right. Once we removed it and re-seated it, the ghosting went away completely.<br /><br />I loaded a photo onto Amazon so you can see what the positioning difference is. You can see it by scrolling up, looking at the main image in the top left, and then clicking through the options for alternate images. Mine is the side by side avatar image. The image on the left is my \\"avatar\\" in a normal Kinect game. On the right is the exact same game, from the exact same location, but now with the Zoom installed. You can even see that I'm holding the camera up. So there's no ghosting or other issues, and the character is definitely much more centered within the available space.<br /><br />I tried a set of games without and then with the Zoom. I got the same scores in the game either way. So my performance wasn't negatively impacted by the Zoom addition. My moves on screen seemed as smooth in either situation.<br /><br />A key here is that the Zoom requires the optics to be perfectly clean and flawless. 030726999X|4.0|This final volume provides a satisfying conclusion to the trilogy, but it might not be the book that fans of the first two in this series expect. In fact, each book in this series actually belongs to a distinct genre--the first is essentially a murder mystery, the second is a blistering psychodrama, and this third one is a crime novel/political thriller/courtroom drama--yet somehow Larsson ties them all together into a coherent and intriguing narrative. The plotting in this one is clever, the characters are well-crafted, and the action moves along at a brisk pace (despite the book's length). I have just two quibbles with this novel--Lisbeth is largely inert through much of the story. Although she utilizes her hacking skills to great effect, she is confined to a hospital bed (and later a prison cell) for the vast majority of the novel. Therefore, if she is indeed \\"The Girl Who Kicked the Hornet's Nest,\\" she must possess some very long legs indeed. And while I'm at it, here's a side quibble--for a series of novels that condemns and reveals (exploits?) violence that men commit against women, why is the title character referred to as a \\"girl\\" throughout this series? She is, in fact, a strong, resourceful, intelligent, and brave woman. The other substantive quibble regards the character of Erika Berger, the once-and-future-editor of Millennium magazine, who is cyberstalked and endangered by a mysterious assailant in a subplot that seems to contribute very little to the main narrative other than highlighting yet another way in which men victimize women. Larsson is a gifted enough storyteller to have woven this subplot more convincingly into the main narrative, and his failure to do so is somewhat disappointing. These two and a half quibbles aside, however, \\"The Girl Who Kicked the Hornet's Nest\\" is a top-notch thriller and a fitting conclusion to the potboiling Millennium trilogy.

B00159679S|5.0|Act II of the Wieland Wagner production of Tannh&auml;user, Bayreuth, 1962<br />Photo  1962 Bayreuth Festival/Decca Classics<br />Back in the '90s (I seem to be starting a lot of articles with that sentence these days) the then-record-company Philips released a gigantic 18\\" long 32-CD box set called the \\"Richard Wagner Edition\\", consisting of live recordings of all ten of the major Wagner operas. (H&ouml;llander, Tannha&uuml;ser, Lohengrin, Tristan, Meistersinger, the whole Ring and Parsifal.<br /><br />It was an expensive set, featuring only recordings made at the Bayreuth Festspielhaus. Unfortunately some of these recordings, made in conjunction with video productions of Wagner's operas, were of mixed and middling quality. Most notably, the Pierre Boulez-led Ring from the late '70s featured grade-A conducting and a grade-C cast. At the same time, older, \\"historical\\" recordings of better quality were issued at premium prices, some of them only available as imports.<br /><br />This new box set, clunkily titled Wagner: The Great Operas of the Bayreuth Festival (weighing in at 33 discs!) is a welcome arrival. Reasonably priced (given its size), this yellow cardboard box eliminate librettos, essays, booklets, jewel boxes and slipcases in favor of simple white envelopes for the discs. Tannh&auml;user, Tristan, Meistersinger. and Parsifal are the same as the earlier set. Let's look at those first.<br /><br />The 1962 Sawallisch recording of Tannh&auml;user features great performances from Wolfgang Windgassen and Grace Bumbry, the mezzo who broke the color barrier at Bayreuth. It is an odd mix of the \\"Dresden\\" and \\"Paris\\" editions of the score. The 1966 Karl B&ouml;hm Tristan pits Windgassen's fiery tenor against the icy power of Birgit Nilsson's soprano. She is not to be messed with. He ain't bad either. Each act fits on a single disc, and was recorded on a separate day. This enabled Windgassen and Nilsson to sing at full blast.<br /><br />The 1974 Meistersinger under Silvio Varviso is a muddle, with a mediocre Walther (Jean Cox) and Eva (Hannelore Bode), and lots of clattery stage noises. However, it boasts a great Hans Sachs from underrated hausbariton Karl Ridderbusch and fine comic timing from the ensemble cast. The final recording to reappear from the old set is the 1985 Parsifal, led at a snail's pace by James Levine is also a mixed bag, capturing an early performance from Waltraud Meier as Kundry (which is good) and a late one from Peter Hoffmann (which is not.). Able support is provided by Hans Sotin (Gurnemanz) and Simon Estes (Amfortas) and the choral singing is excellent. However, the best Bayreuth Parsifal remains the 1962 live recording under Hans Knappertsbutsch.<br /><br />Now for the good stuff. This set flanks the 1962 Sawallisch Tannha&uuml;ser nicely by including his generally excellent recordings of Der Fliegende H&ouml;llander (1961) and Lohengrin (1962). All three feature Swedish soprano Anja Silja, who was still a teenager when she sang the leading roles of Senta, Elisabeth and Elsa at the Festspielhaus. (It didn't hurt matters either that she was the girlfriend of Festival co-director Wieland Wagner, the composer's grandson.)<br /><br />Silja is incandescent in the Lohengrin, just one jewel in an amazing cast that features Franz Crass, Ramon Vinay, supersoprano Astrid Varnay (the scariest Ortrud ever!) and Jess Thomas as Lohengrin. Best of all, this is one of those \\"just stand there\\" Wieland Wagner productions, so there is almost no stage noise, just great singing.<br /><br />Finally, the new Wagner box includes the complete 1967 Karl B&ouml;hm Ring. Once again, the set features Nilsson and Windgassen as Brunnhilde and Siegfried. And yes, their chemistry is real, and even better here than on the studio-recorded Solti Ring with the Vienna Philharmonic. But the real treasure of this excellent, energetic Ring is the performance of Leonie Rysanek as Sieglinde in Die Walk&uuml;re. This Walk&uuml;re sets the gold standard for this opera, complete with the famous, theatrically orgasmic scream (a Wieland addition) at the end of the first act.<br /><br />The Siegfried is one of the best readings of Wagner's fairy tale. Windgassen's unflagging energy a wonder to behold. B&ouml;hm, a Strauss protege and a man of the theater, shows his bespectacled brilliance on the podium, bringing the final bars of G&ouml;tterd&auml;mmerung home in a blaze of orchestral color.<br /><br />(Note to Wagner nuts: Yes, this is the same controversial production of the Ring in which Wieland cut Gutrune's short scene out of Act III of G&ouml;tterd&auml;mmerung. However the performance on these discs includes the missing scene, restored to its proper place.)famous, theatrically orgasmic scream (a Wieland addition) at the end of the first act.<br /><br />The Siegfried is one of the best readings of Wagner's fairy tale. Windgassen's unflagging energy a wonder to behold. Bhm, a Strauss protege and a man of the theater, shows his bespectacled brilliance on the podium, bringing the final bars of Gtterdmmerung home in a blaze of orchestral color.<br /><br />(Note to Wagner nuts: Yes, this is the same controversial production of the Ring in which Wieland cut Gutrune's short scene out of Act III of Gtterdmmerung. However the performance on these discs includes the missing scene, restored to its proper place.)

1400052173|5.0|I had actually known the basic outlines of the HeLa story for years: the discovery of a strange, aggressive form of cervical cancer in a young and healthy black woman in segregated 1950s Virginia, the inability to treat her case, and the research built from her cells after she died -- tough and practically immortal, and a major part of biological research in the years since (to the point of HeLa actually becoming a weed in some other cell cultures). But Rebecca Skloot went to find out the family's side of the story, and in the process created a new classic. Skloot puts a different spin on it; she crosses the line of becoming the story and in the process tells the story of a family swept along by history, proud of Lacks' contribution to science but justifiably angry at the fact that it took them twenty years to find out about the bits of Henrietta that remained alive. Skloot is in general an excellent writer with a strong sense of the personal; although her Mark Twain-esques attempt to represent the Lacks family's AAVE dialect seem strange and jarring, her ups and downs (mostly ups) as she gets to know the family play a part in the story as well, but not so much as to distract from the facts.<br /><br />Overall, this story is justly a classic, both of science writing and biography of a family that has gone through the years with no control over the memory of their mother, whose life contributed one of the greatest discoveries in medical history. Skloot does an excellent job of capturing it, and this book has justly earned all the interest it's recieved since it came out.

They didn't like the choices of characters she killed off, it felt rushed, Katniss wasn't the same, Why Prim? what happened to Gale? I was so confused, because all of these questions and concerns had answers in my mind, answers that made perfect sense.<br /><br />One of the huge problems I've seen that people have with how this book played out, is they feel there isn't enough time to process the deaths. They just happen, and well, that's it. They were upset that they didn't have time to mourn with Katniss, and that there weren't paragraphs dedicated to each of them. All I could think was: this is a war they're fighting. In war, you don't have time to lock yourself away for months to mourn the death of your comrades. All the sacrifices her fellow friends and soldiers made were not in vane. They were made so she could continue on and change the world. They too had loved ones living in this world, they sacrificed for them as well. Katniss lives the rest of her life with the weight and the guilt of their deaths. If the rest of your life isn't enough time to come to terms and mourn, well, I don't know what to tell you.<br /><br />As for it being sloppy and rushed, I didn't feel that at all. The quickness of it helped me realize the urgency of their mission. Katniss had taken over a part of the army and was leading her own mini rebellion. She was unorganized, and had to move, think, and act very quickly, or else the entire plan fall apart.<br /><br />And as for Katniss not being our same fiery Katniss, I can more than understand that too. She had seen and been responsible for countless deaths. Faces she'd never seen. The first arena alone haunted and changed her. A full blown war, with her at the center? It's a miracle she didn't implode. She watched all those she loved twisted and changed before her eyes. Gale was becoming a war monster, his rebellious streak growing and taking over. Prim was older, more independent, less afraid. She was facing a terrible world and doing it without Katniss. Peeta was turned into a ghost of himself, and she couldn't bear losing her \\"boy with the bread.\\" Katniss had been deeply affected and hurt by all that her world had done to her, and all she had done to others. She was realizing she was little more than a pawn to both sides, and that shook her.<br /><br />I think given the circumstances, this book ends very happily, and there are very very few loose ends, if any! I have yet to find any, except maybe what happened to Joanna Mason. She goes back to her life and she moves on. She never forgets the people who died. Peeta returns. The friendship between Katniss and Gale had long been severed, the incident with Prim was just the final blow. It was a small one, there was no real evidence as to whether he had anything to do with it, but it was enough. Katniss grows up, has children of her own, and learns to live and love again. I had no problems thinking it was a beautiful ending for a fractured girl and boy in a broken world. They were safe now, that was the goal all along. They'd finally kept each other safe.

0307957934|2.0|Most of the reviews here focus on Harris' 21st century take on HAL and the lack of appealing characters (other than perhaps LeClerq). As for the AI computer run amok trope, yes it has been done a million times before and better. On the other hand, the rather unsympathetic characters were definitely by design. I think Harris' problem was trying to reconcile the requirements of a potboiler with the desire to reproach the financial services industry and explore the negative effect it has had on science and the overall economy in recent decades. Hoffman is a symbol more than a character, representative of the underfunding of science (particularly stateside) and the brain drain that financial services has brought to the scientific community as opportunity and easy money attract the best and brightest away from scientific endeavor and towards the self-serving arena of high finance. He also represents the pursuit of a personal goal (in his case, AI) regardless of its impact on other people. He is a cipher who seems to lack empathy. Quarry represents the self-absorbed, shallow, if not a bit sociopathological side of the business world, totally willing to keep the system going as long as it benefits him and with no regard to the danger it poses to others. The third indictment is of course, aimed at the high-speed computer trading schemes that create unfair advantages to the traders that employ them, as well as how a significant percentage of the finance industry no longer serves a broader purpose in the economy and has simply become a parasite.<br /><br />All valid and timely points, but I think Harris' attempt to execute a suspenseful, sci-fi lite thriller around these criticisms ultimately fails because there is a lack of human connection and a plot resolution that is too obvious early on. With enough real-world examples of malfeasance to call upon, these themes are perhaps more easily explored via nonfiction.

1449305172|4.0|I figure that if I get more than one usable idea out of a 'how to' book then I am doing well. The other message? Focus on the customer.<br /><br />None of that is new, but what I did find new was an approach to business planning and metric monitoring that gets all the important stuff onto one page. While written for software startups, the book has lots of value for anyone trying to get a new business up and running (I'm starting a non-software company and the book has been a godsend).<br /><br />I would have given this book five stars but it is lumpy. Some points are labored, the obvious is stated a few times too many, and it is a bit heavy on the aconyms..<br /><br />But these are minor flaws in a book that is perhaps most marked by the obvious excitement of the author for his topic. As for the three things I learnt? Two have been mentioned (the one page plan and metrics - truly great) and the third was some sensible stuff startegies to follow as you  get your product to a market fit.<br /><br />If you are planning a software start up then buy this book - my guess is that you will learn something (if not you can feel comfortable in your pre-existing well rounded knowledge). If you are planning a start-up in another field then this is still worth a read. As I indicated above, I'm starting a new business (a small group tour company) and while much of the book wasn't relevant to the specifics of that business, there was enough there to justify the cover price and time taken to read it.

B000001FXR|5.0|CATCH A FIRE is the second mainstream album release for The Wailers. Contrary to popular belief, their first album released outside of Jamaica was SOUL REBEL in 1970.  This, however, would be the album were Reggae truly arrived & the masses took notice. This was the album that inspired singers/songwriters around the world, both known and unknown, to learn how to write & perform Roots & Ska Reggae.  The bands primary members, Bob Marley, Peter Tosh & Bunny Wailer, along with Aston Barrett, Carlton Barrett & John Bundrick,  were about to embark on a truly historic run that would bring the poetic, revolutionary & spiritual nature of Reggae to an audience that had never heard anything quite like that before.<br /><br />Although the entire band worked wonderfully together as a cohesive unit, this was very much an album that was dominated by the musical genius of Bob Marley who wrote every song with the exception of 2 which were written by Peter Tosh. However, one of those songs by Tosh, \\"400 years\\", is one of my personal favorites on the album. Take a listen to that one and reflect as it's pure lyrical & musical mastery! Overall one of the most important albums in music history as it was the beginnging of one of the most influential periods of the 20th century.<br /><br />CATCH A FIRE was the culmination of years of hard work were The Wailers took an island genre, honed it's sound & message, into a reflection of the injustices and hardships they were experiencing in Jamaica & other island nations & made it mainstream. Reggae as a particular genre of music now generates billions of dollars worldwide and The Wailers are one of the primary reasons why.  I think one of the most underappreciated things about this band is it's musicianship.<br /><br />The albums first track, \\"Concrete Jungle\\" has a gorgeous introduction with an almost blues rock feel to it and the guitar solo is spectacular.  The variety on the album is wonderful, from the hard driving songs of opression like this song, \\"Slave Driver\\" and the aforementioned \\"400 years\\" to a song of courtship and desire like \\"Baby We've Got A Date\\" that has almost a Country music feel with the slide guitar.  This was a time of great revolution for around the world for oppressed peoples to be able to live, love, worship and express how they felt according to their own conscious.  The island nations were fighting for freedom from their european oppressors as were many of the African nations with whom The Wailers shared a kindred spirit with and their songs were the songs of freedom, in all aspects of life, for many of those nations and became a ralling cry as well.<br /><br />These were the times of movements such as \\"Black Power\\" & \\"Civil Rights\\" here in the U.S. and many historical artists and bands were picking up their pens and writing songs of revolution. The sheer poetry of groups like The Impressions, Earth, Wind and Fire, Sly and the Family Stone and artists like Marvin Gaye, Stevie Wonder and James Brown, were singing about those of African heritage rising up and solidifying their power and spear heading a revolution of sorts here too.<br /><br />It's interesting that The Wailers patterned their group after The Impressions with a rich poetic vibe along with a gospel or Spiritual message just under the surface. This is a clear portrait of what made this band great and historic. The Wailers who were truly about to shake the very foundations of the world in a very short period of time had, by this time, developed a tight sound to go with their lyrical content.<br /><br />The Wailers were blessed with three very talented and deeply spiritual songwriters in Marley, Tosh and Neville O'Riley Livingston aka Bunny Wailer. The music they made as a part of this band and the music produced after their break up are some of the most beloved at any point in music history. This was truly a special time as the world was changing at a rapid pace and no one had ever heard of this type of music, coming from this point of view before at least not on a \\"mainstream\\" level.<br /><br />This is why today The Wailers and especially Bob Marley are so very beloved and admired by countless millions around the world. Their songs were songs of revolution and not just in a physical since but also mental and spiritual as well. Their will never be another band like them and those of us who were blessed to witness their greatness while together have never and will never be the same.g from this point of view before at least not on a \\"mainstream\\" level.<br /><br />This is why today The Wailers and especially Bob Marley are so very beloved and admired by countless millions around the world. B005YVTHT0|4.0|First thing you'll need to know is that if you've used other wheels (the kind with a fixed base), this one will take some getting used to.  It's not attached to anything but a long, sturdy USB chord and relies on accelerometers for sensing input, like a smartphone or a wiimote.  This was a little jarring at first, because the wheel doesn't rotate around a fixed axis so feeling position isn't always the easiest.<br /><br />However, the after playing with your sensitivity settings and tweaking it, the accelerometers do a hell of a job giving you precise, immediate control.  Obviously there's no force feedback or any of that, it would have been nice to have some sort of vibration though, but not crucial.  The paddles on the back, which take care of acceleration and braking, are also very precise, though I wish they had a stronger or adjustable spring return, just for a little more positive pressure.<br /><br />There are loads of buttons on this thing, and they're set up for one specific racing title (Simraceways), which admittedly I didn't play too much of.  Most of my driving was in F1 2010/2011 and Dirt 3.  Since the buttons are labeled it was pretty easy to translate which ones I wanted to handle which tasks in those games.<br /><br />Overall, though, the thing looks pretty cool.  Honestly it's a tad embarrassing to have a giant force feedback wheel sitting near your desk, they're bulky and get in the way.  This little guy can go up on a shelf when not in use and just looks like racing memorabilia which is a nice little bonus.  I do wish it came with a rotating stand, though, something it could mount to when you're playing a game to give you that fixed axis that most racing gamers are used to.

006113127X|3.0|Factotum (1975), by Charles Bukowski (1920-1994)<br /><br />The novel was author Bukowski's second, and it centers on the degenerate life of Hank Chinaski, Bukowski's alter-ego. The story traces Hank as he consumes copious amounts of alcohol, interviews and changes jobs with regularity, either through quitting or being fired--I lost count of how many--and screws women, most of whom are alcoholic whores. A stark review might be: Having to work sucks but it finances the drinking; and women are necessary for sexual gratification.<br /><br />This is not a pleasant book. It describes skid row life around World War II in places like Los Angeles, St. Louis, New Orleans, and Miami as failed writer Hank Chinaski moves from job to job and rented room to rented room, mostly drunk or with a hangover--with classical music in the background. What is pleasurable about the book isn't the story; it's Bukowski's ability to develop the characters and his writing style. The fact that I didn't like Chinaski--but wished he'd straighten out so I could like him--suggests that the author knew how to paint him. Examples:<br /><br />\\"I got into bed, opened the bottle, worked the pillow into a hard knot behind my back, took a deep breath, and sat in the dark looking out the window. It was the first time I had been alone for five days. I was a man who thrived on solitude; without it I was like another man without food or water. Each day without solitude weakened me. I took no pride in my solitude; but I was dependent on it. The Darkness of the room was like sunlight to me. I took a drink of wine.\\"<br /><br />\\"That was all a man needed: hope. It was the lack of hope that discouraged a man. I remembered my New Orleans days, living on two five-cent candy bars a day for weeks at a time in order to have leisure to write. But starvation, unfortunately, didn't improve art. It only hindered it. A man's soul was rooted in his stomach. A man could write much better after eating a porterhouse steak and drinking a pint of whisky than he could ever write after eating a nickel candy bar. The myth of the starving artist is a hoax. Once you realized that everything was a hoax you got wise and began to bleed and burn your fellow man....\\"<br /><br />\\"When I got back to Los angeles I found a cheap hotel...and I stayed in bed and drank. I drank for some time, three or four days. I couldn't get myself to read the want ads. The thought of sitting in front of a man behind a desk and telling him that I wanted a job, that I was qualified for a job, was too much for me. Frankly, I was horrified by life, at what a man had to do simply in order to eat, sleep, and keep himself clothed. So I stayed in bed and drank. When you drank the world was still out there, but for the moment it didn't have you by the throat.\\"<br /><br />A conversation with his live-in girlfriend:<br /><br />Jan: \\"We sat here all day and evening yesterday. You told me about your parents. Your parents hated you. Right?\\"<br /><br />Hank: \\"Right.\\"<br /><br />Jan: \\"So now you're a little crazy. No love. Everybody needs love. It's warped you.\\"<br /><br />Hank: \\"People don't need love. What they need is success in one form or another. It can be love but it needn't be.\\"<br /><br />Jan: \\"The Bible says, `Love thy neighbor.'\\"<br /><br />Hank: \\"That could mean to leave him alone....\\"<br /><br />At a job:<br /><br />\\"I wasn't very good. My idea was to wander about doing nothing, always avoiding the boss, and avoiding the stoolies who might report to the boss. I wasn't all that clever. It was more instinct than anything else. I always started a job with the feeling that I'd soon quit or be fired, and this gave me a relaxed manner that was mistaken for intelligence or some secret power.\\"<br /><br />Later, at another job:<br /><br />\\"...There were always men looking for jobs in America. There were always all these usable bodies. And I wanted to be a writer. Not everybody thought they could be a dentist or an automobile mechanicbut everybody knew they could be a writer....But most men, fortunately aren't writers, or even cab drivers, and some men--many men--unfortunately aren't anything.\\"<br /><br />And my favorite line: \\"Every man is a poet.\\"<br /><br />I liked this book for the reasons expressed, but I must admit I wouldn't want a steady diet of Bukowski. I will read more of his work--in occasional sprinkles, over time.<br /><br />For reference, a factotum is a handyman, a jack of all trades; assistant, man Friday, gal/girl Friday; gofer; or as Wikipedia says, \\"...a general servant or a person having many diverse activities or responsibilities. 0312576463|3.0|I am generally not sucked in by hype. I have no great need to see the latest movies as soon as they premier or view all the films nominated for best picture. I don't have to read the hottest book on the market, right away, so I can talk about it, appearing \\"with it\\" to those into the latest books (particularly a book that our dear leader, Prince Fluffy Bunny, selected as something to read). Nonetheless, I was intrigued by the subject title--how freedom would be treated--and in recognition that Franzen has built a celebrated reputation in literary circles, I got into the queue at the public library, some 260 down on the list, and waited.<br /><br />It was worth the wait...sort of.<br /><br />The New York Times book review of August 19, 2010 called it \\"a masterpiece of American fiction.\\" the Los Angeles Times book review of August 20, 2010 called it a \\"rich and nuanced novel.\\" And the Guardian-Observer book review of September 19, 2010 said it's \\"funny and poignant\\" adding a cautionary note that  it's \\"only trouble is the weight of expectation it carries\\" due to Franzen's prior success in 2001 with Corrections. (In contrast, there are a lot of negative reviews at amazon.com.) I enjoyed the book--it was difficult to put down. Its weird characters and their trials will be with me for a long time. But therein lies the problem: While Franzen uses a lot of big words (a sign that he's \\"literary\\"?), and the interesting device of having one of the main characters use \\"autobiography\\" to carry the story, everyone in the book is dysfunctional. There doesn't seem to be a normal person in it. I know there are those that would like these characters because of their sexual proclivities and their drug use (Charlie Sheen comes to mind). I was left with an empty feeling about them.<br /><br />My publisher had a comment in an e-mail in reference to my novel, (which I'm editing again) that I'll quote--and we weren't even discussing Freedom: \\"...there's a reason why there's so much out-ethics and angst-ridden crap these days in novels....that's what the buying public is picking up (but I wonder if it's just b/c that's what they're fed.) I believe that there are enough decent people out there who want to read decent literature, that I say carry on! \\" Yes, \\"angst-ridden crap\\" is a pretty good description.<br /><br />And how was freedom dealt with? If you're one of those--liberal, leftist, conservative, or reactionary, it matters not which--that think the world simply must be organized into a moral structure that satisfies your utopian impulses, then freedom is something that must be restrained. As if to say,\\"We can't have too many people actually doing what they want and living like they want, now can we? These freedom lovers have it too good--must reign them in to the new rules, organized by philosopher kings of our choosing.\\" So, if this is your basic assumption, there is plenty in the book that will support it--we have too much freedom, which apparently kills a lot of birds.<br /><br />On the other hand, if you are one who sees freedom as mainly about sex, then there is plenty in the book to suggest that freedom is good, which is to say that there is a lot of screwing  going on; and even more thinking about screwing. The author does, however, suggest that there are some pesky consequences...because, you know...people have feelings about such things.<br /><br />Franzen never quite says so, but the freedom bit seems to be presented as a problem to be solved and the social forces for \\"good\\" need to solve it--by what? He doesn't say; but he does leave the impression that it must be solved (and we know who he thinks the social forces for good are). In my view, if it's a problem, it's a problem of an individual choosing how one shall live their life. Freedom is like arriving at a complex intersection that you have to navigate your way through knowing full well that you might take a wrong path, but you make your choices to the best of your ability and live (or die) with the consequences. You may have to backtrack and try a different path, if you're able. Not everybody does this well, all the time--a decent balance of good decisions and minor mistakes is the best most of us can achieve. That's not a problem; it's an opportunity to learn who you are.<br /><br />To close, what follows is a poem by Anthony Madrid, who \\"has written a paradox, calls it the paradox of the leash. To solve it, you have to establish who's at the freer end of the strap.\\" Is this what Franzen is saying? Life is a bitch because of freedom?<br /><br />I Have Passed Too Many Years Among Cool<br />Designing Beings<br /><br />I have passed too many years among cool, designing beings.<br />I have contracted their reptile manner in my soul.<br /><br />Last night I lay awake, judging the earth and all its creatures.<br />My dozen angry blood cells were frowning in the jury box.<br /><br />Having nothing to say, I said nothing a long time. But now,<br />Humiliated anger presses me cruelly ...<br /><br />I sketch a human hand: I leave the outline open-ended. 0307352145|2.0|There are several books on this topic listed on Amazon.  I haven't seen one yet that I found impressive.  In my opinion, C G Jung, who invented the disnction between extraversion and intraversion, and went on to develop a theory of types based on that distinction plus a theory of four psychological functions, and the Meyer-Briggs and &#34;Please Understand Me&#34; people who popularized the theory, covered this topic very well.  Certainly there's more that can be added, but I don't undersetand writing a whole book about introversion and not covering the rest of Jung's theory of types. Jung developed the concept of introvert/extravert to explain to himself the differences between himself and Freud; in his work after that, he came to realize that that wasn't enough, so he went on to develop the theory of the four functions.  I think anyone interested in the topic should read Jung, or at least his popularizers.  Evidently, people have heard of introversion, tend to equate it with shyness or social awkwardness, see it as a problem, and want help with it; but they aren't aware of the existence of the functions of intuition, sensing, thinking and feeling (as defined by Jung).  I would hope that that would be the least that a book on introversion would do, to introduce these concepts, but noooo....  I reviewed one of the other books on the topic, and while 60% or more of the readers found the review helpful, a few people gave me serious heck for daring to criticize that book, for suggesting that it should have covered the basic information on the topic, and for including some information myself.  Years later, I was still catching flak--only from a few people it's true, but I finally had to stop following that discussion.  I see some of the same going on here; this book is not as bad as that one, but has the same problems, and like that other book, it has gotten lots of glowing reviews, and the few critical reviews are subjected to criticism.  Here are the problems I see with this book, and apparently most of the others:  --There's not much solid, useful information, and the basics are not covered.  Intraversion/extraversion is only part of the story.  If you're an intuitive introvert, for example you may have more common with an intuitive extravert than a sensing type who happens to be introverted.  There are also degrees of introversion and degrees of the other characteristics.  Of course, Jung's theory is not necessarily the last word on the subject, but it's pretty much the first word, and most of the ones in the middle, and you really can't do the subject justice by just taking part of his theory and ignoring the rest.  --The popular books on this subject, at least the ones I've read lately or seen on Amazon, are &#34;self-help&#34; books.  They assume that introversion is a defect or a problem, and try to help you feel better about yourself. It's true that some people, especially those with rare types like the Meyer-Briggs types INFJ or INFP, have problems associated with their type, and learning something about these matters can be a big help.  That certainly was true for me.  Just being an introvert doesn't make you that rare, though, and isn't necessarily such a huge problem, or even a problem at all.  There's no need to whine about it, or to be told &#34;there, there&#34;, for a whole book. This book, on the whole, seems to assume that introversion is a problem, and much of the book is about how to deal with it, mostly by sucking it up and acting like an extravert.  There are some good points made in the book; for example, that shyness and introversion are not the same thing.  Shyness is painful, introversion, not necessarily.  Shyness is a disability, introversion, no, or not necessarily.  The point I'd want to make though, and this again is Jung's discovery, is that if you're an introvert, you tend to get in trouble when you act like an extravert, or you're expected to but you don't know how-- and vice versa.  If you're anintuitive type, it's your inferior function of sensing that tends to let you down or get you in trouble.  Everyone has some introversion, some extraversion, and some of each of the four functions in their makeup.  We tend to be better at, more conscious of, more identified with, certain aspects of our personalities.  It's developing, bring to awareness, the most unconscious parts that really yields results.  Suppose your type, introvert or extravert, thinking or feeling, sensing or intuitive, isn't a problem? Is there any reason then to be interested in this topic?  Well, yeaahh--The Keirsey book is called &#34;Please Understand Me&#34;.  Understanding your own type, and other types, and how different types interact and communicate, or don't communicate, it's just kind of interesting, isn't it?  And it can help us function better, understand other people better, without having to come from a ground of being defective.  We can, maybe, be happier and more productive, even though we're okay to start with.  Jung's concept of individuation was inextricably tied up with his theory of types.  One of the main things you need to do is identify your inferior function (for example, I'm an extreme intuitive, so intuition is my superior function, and sensing is my inferior function), and that's what you need to work on.  I suggest that developing your inferior function is much more important than worrying about being an introvert-- whether you want to become more of the person you can be, or you just want to get fixed. --There is some rather questionable neuroscience in this book-- unsupported, of dubious accuracy, and irrelevant anyway.  Even if it were true, say, that extraversion or introversion has something to do with the amygdala, or how you process dopamine, so what?  How do you translate that into understanding of your own personality, or into action? --There's quite a bit of American history in the book, which I find interesting, but the history is only a little more impressive, and probably no more relevant, than the neuroscience. --There's a section, supposedly one of the &#34;parts&#34; of the book but actually containing only one chapter, enitled &#34;Do all cultures have an extravert ideal&#34;?, but it don't talk about all cultures.  It talks about a few Asian-Americans in the Bay area, in general or anecdotal terms.  I guess the answer to the question is &#34;no&#34;, since Finns  and Scandinavians are notoriously introverted, and then there are those Asians; but it doesn't really talk in any detail about any introverted culture; it's mostly just buying into the idea that this culture is extraverted, and being introverted is a problem.  The theory of types, particularly as popularized in Keirsey's &#34;Please Understand Me&#34; and the Meyer-Briggs test, is something very interesting, and potentially very useful, for a lot of people. (Some people don't get it, or don't like it.  People who are pretty much balanced in extraversion/introversion, sensing/intuition, etc. tend not to understand what's being talked about; sensing types tend to hate it.  To me that's evidence for the validity of the theory: it can predict who won't like it)!  But the theory of types is not everything, and it's not that simple.  It's worth studying if you're interested, but I think you no need to go into it deeper than this book does.

B000002JPA|4.0|Dream Theater's second album sees the band considerately sharpening their traditional progressive metal approach, which combines the theatrical thematic portrayal of Queen, the rhythmic complexity and structural dynamic of Rush, a restrained style of Metallica-like speed metal riffing, and the dramatic approach to melodic construction of Marillion to arrive at a distinct sound easily recognizable as the band's own. Typical of progressive rock/metal, this album features quite a few extended instrumental sections during which the musicians display formidable technical skill in complex instrumental interactions, but what sets Dream Theater apart from most is that for the most part these sections are not dynamic diversions from the song's central theme, but elaborations of particular events of that defining theme presented in dramatic form, as part of a coherent development towards harmonic resolution of the song's textural tension and thematic conflict.<br /><br />\\"This world is spinning around me<br />The whole world keeps<br />spinning around me<br />All life is future to past<br />Every breath leaves me one less<br />to my last\\"<br /><br />The instrumentation is of a high level of technical skill, with each member showing excellent proficiency at his instrument, and the band shows understanding of theory application within richly textured and multi-sectioned compositions; these are very skilled players and there are lots of guitar and keyboard solos that occasionally fall into a common tendency of self-indulgence, but when this band is honing in on a unified musical theme progressing toward realization of a song's conceptual culmination, it produces outstanding results in the sheer delight of the technical excellence orchestrated to fulfill these grand designs. To realize these expansive themes of perseverance and hope, Dream Theater structure songs to contrast shades of light and dark, emphasizing the overcoming of struggle through a symbolic triumph of positive change and illumination in the form of ascending melodic guitars and shining keyboards, gliding smoothly over a rhythmic pattern established by precise and aware drumming, and fantastically fluid and flexible bass guitar, working frequent and unexpected time-changes to re-route thematic development in a way that varies a given mood, something that this band does very well: establishing clear and powerful atmospheric mood despite the substantial technical display of instruments and intricate patterns, assisted by a vocalist significantly skilled in dramatic emotional representations of the leading thematic ideas, in the high-register vocal style common to traditional heavy metal as formulated in the 1970s and 1980s, and though he sometimes tests the stratospheric heights of his range with a little too much theatrical flair, his performance intensely enhances the expressive presence and force of the band's music. The disc sounds spectacular and there are a number of inspirational and uplifting moments throughout this album, which is the band's best and one of the most definitive of the style, but might have been better without the tendency to shape melodies in ordinary pop-music styles, which while giving this album a more accessible quality than the average progressive metal affair, also weakens the band's otherwise potent expressive character, particularly in songs like \\"Another Day\\" and some overly sentimental moments in \\"Surrounded\\", but compositions like \\"Metropolis - Part 1\\", \\"Under a Glass Moon\\", and \\"Learning to Live\\" show Dream Theater at their best, offering a variety of diverse stylistic elements while combining top-notch technical musicianship with enveloping atmosphere and pure feeling.

B006IDG2T6|1.0|As with the other 1 and 2 star reviews, I simply find Mr. Harris' work in this book to be lacking in character and honesty.  Free will is a philosophical concept that cannot with such simplicity be dismissed by physical evidence, scant or voluminous.  To put a fine point on it, if the scientific evidence can in fact prove that alpha waves associated (by the thinker) with a given thought precede the thought itself, this still does not disprove the existence of the thought itself as an original creation of the physical mechanism which generated and interpreted it.  Further, to suppose that experience alone negates choice (or personal responsibility for choice) is juvenile.  What, if not the specific physical mechanism of the individual brain can make any interpretation or use of such experiences?<br /><br />I suppose the largest problem I have with Mr. Harris' argument is simply that it reduces all of human existence and creativity down to a poorly disguised game of crooked dice, one which has no creator at the helm (with which I cannot argue) but one that is crooked none-the-less and which can only be blamed upon the cosmos.  The universe is cheating, but we still don't have any choice.  Fate exists, while simultaneously being without source and without direction.

B005FVA3LK|5.0|First of all, I should confess- I'm old.  I was there.  I was there for the complete inertia that Achtung Baby represented.  For those of us who didn't hate Rattle and Hum, but contrarily loved it and were eager to see what they would do next, Achtung Baby was as shocking to hear as it must have been difficult to make.  I was initially stumped, confounded, even frustrated by Achtung Baby, but it has since grown into my favorite Rock Album of all time.  And it remains, to this day, one of the best musical discoveries, and musical experiences, of my life.<br /><br />I tell you that to say, I was going to be a tough sell for this box- if only because I snatched up, and still have, every Single from the record, including Imports... and I thought I pretty much had everything a fan could collect, or obsessively hunt down, from that era.  What was a new box going to offer me, that I didn't already have?  If that's you, and I know I'm not alone in this... This box is everything we could have dreamed it would be!<br /><br />I know that's contrary to some reviewers who say that they wish there were more, or different, content.  But I'm prepared to tell you that if you are a die-hard fan of this band and this album, and Rock music in general, the material that debuts here is worth the price of the box, even if you already have the rest of the music.  And, let me say, you didn't already have these remasters- of both Achtung Baby and Zooropa, and also of those remixes and b-sides.  They sound a-mazing.  But there is yet more exclusive material included here...<br /><br />It is:<br />-Some Recent Remixes (I don't recall ever hearing \\"One\\" remixed before now... Covered, maybe, but not remixed.  \\"Real Thing\\" and \\"Mysterious Ways\\" also get updated remix treatment)<br />-Exclusive B-Sides (\\"Blow Your House Down,\\" \\"Heaven and Hell,\\" \\"Oh Berlin,\\" \\"Near The Island,\\" \\"Everybody Loves A Winner,\\" and \\"Down All The Days\\" -an alternate treatment of \\"Numb\\")<br />-From The Sky Down (of course,it debuted on disc here)<br />-Several tracks on The Videos DVD (like the footage from Stop Sellafield) are exclusive to this release<br />-The entire \\"Bonus Material\\" disc (If you're like me, you've only had a VHS of the ZooTV special since 1992... SO psyched to see it here!)<br />...and the piece de resistance:<br />-The KINDERGARTEN Version of Achtung Baby<br /><br />Yes, to me, this latter entry is worth the price of the box, all by itself... even if you weren't getting all that other stuff.  The Kindergarten Version reminds me of what it felt like to listen hard, with famished curiosity, to this mad explosion coming out of the speakers- and, in both cases, then and now, the reward was the same: I was winding my way into Achtung Baby.  For those of us who had the experience I described above, this Kindergarten Version is the closest new experience I could ever imagine having that replicates the old, former one.  It's a really special gift.  I've never seen a band do something like this, even though you hear \\"demo\\" versions and \\"early\\" versions all the time.  This is a true 'alternate' record.  The Achtung Baby that almost was.  The songs are still there, with a great many twists and ideas you've never heard inside those musical frameworks until now.  It may not strike you the same way, if you didn't have the experience I did in 1991-2... 0307719219|5.0|The authors provide a strongly descriptive, but not particularly prescriptive, discussion about what they believe are key determinants of the success or failure of societies.  The prescriptive part is difficult because it may be impossible to direct events and individuals toward making the \\"right\\" choices in their political and economic systems.  Acemoglu and Robinson make a very strong case for their theory that a stable government with what they call \\"inclusive\\" political and economic systems produces long-term prosperity and success, while \\"extractive\\" systems run by corrupt elites destroy initiative and at best limit growth and at worst result in complete failures of a nation.  Perhaps the most engaging and enjoyable aspect of this treatise is the big and little supportive stories drawn from pivotal times in the history of many nations and people - ancient to modern - which make for wonderful reading to anyone with an interest in history and its lessons.  In other words, these vignettes are worth the read quite apart from the important ideas they illustrate.  This book helps the reader understand the positive impacts of the Roman and Napoleonic empires, chronic economic problems in Latin America and Africa, the reasons the industrial revolution occurred in some countries while being resisted in others, why development in communist countries ultimately stalls out, and how seemingly small events can have huge, pivotal, surprising consequences.  This book is not just for political science and economics students.  It conveys lessons extremely pertinent to today's economic and political discussions which we would do well to heed.  It also reminds us that successful societies are the exception, not the rule, and are not guaranteed to flourish indefinitely.
